    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="527e1a5006b542438c192810bc05cd3b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '84bddbd5-c711-40b3-a180-9b1226f86cf7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d123539665" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>In the designed “salt-in-polymer” gel-polymer electrolyte, dissolved NaF salt occupies free solvents leading to concentrated-electrolyte environment and added aggregates, which induced stable inorganic-rich interphase. It delivers enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics from electrolyte to interface and high compatibility with different cathodes and anodes. The assembled solid-state Na || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cells display excellent electrochemical performance at all-climate conditions.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9a667b1a-6dcd-4780-bda3-65ccb07f092f/anie202402245-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9a667b1a-6dcd-4780-bda3-65ccb07f092f/anie202402245-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/89f2b1e2-2066-405f-bd59-5cc9d0f3a148/anie202402245-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9a667b1a-6dcd-4780-bda3-65ccb07f092f/anie202402245-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d123539668" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Gel-polymer electrolyte (GPE) is a pragmatic choice for high-safety sodium batteries but still plagued by interfacial compatibility with both cathode and anode simultaneously. Here, salt-in-polymer fibers with NaF salt inlaid in polylactide (PLA) fiber network was fabricated via electrospinning and subsequent in situ forming gel-polymer electrolyte in liquid electrolytes. The obtained PLA-NaF GPE achieves a high ion conductivity (2.50×10<sup>−3</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>) and large Na<sup>+</sup> transference number (0.75) at ambient temperature. Notably, the dissolution of NaF salt occupies solvents leading to concentrated-electrolyte environment, which facilitates aggregates with increased anionic coordination (anion/Na<sup>+</sup> &gt;1). Aggregates with higher HOMO realize the preferential oxidation on the cathode so that inorganic-rich and stable CEI covers cathode’ surface, preventing particles’ breakage and showing good compatibility with different cathodes (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.32</sub>Mn<sub>0.68</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>). While, passivated Na anode induced by the lower LUMO of aggregates, and the lower surface tension between Na anode and PLA-NaF GPE interface, leading to the dendrites-free Na anode. As a result, the assembled Na || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cells display excellent electrochemical performance at all-climate conditions.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202402245-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202402245-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Increasing demands for efficient energy storage call for larger-scale electrochemical storage devices. Sodium-based batteries are one of the preferred systems owning to the resource abundance and similar preparation process to lithium-based batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0001_R_d123539657e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature">1</a>, <a href="#anie202402245-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0002_R_d123539657e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> While for improve energy density at the system level above 500 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, innovations in electrolyte aspect are urgent to be exploited. However, conventional liquid electrolyte (LE) faces serious safety issues by the reason that the inflammable and uncontrollable parasitic reactions in electrode/electrolyte interface. Then, this problem pushes researchers’ attention to the solidification of electrolytes. Ceramic electrolytes could provide high ion conductivity at ambient temperature,<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0003_R_d123539657e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun.">3</a></sup></span> but they present poor processability and unstable interfacial chemistry, which hinders their application in normal temperature and pressure at this stage. Consequently, the researchers’ focus has shifted to polymer electrolytes. While, gel-polymer electrolyte (GPE) combined with plasticizer molecules and flexible polymer chains has emerged as an optimal option, which possess exceptional processability and designability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0004_R_d123539657e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Commun.">4</a>-<a href="#anie202402245-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0007_R_d123539657e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Energy">7</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Unfortunately, GPEs typically suffer from unideal ion transport kinetics (low ion conductivity and ion transference number, σ<sub>Li+</sub>=σ×t<sub>Li+</sub>) and interfacial compatibility at ambient temperature. Originating from the strong coupling between ion conduction and the mobility of polymer chain segments, their practical application is strongly prohibited, especially encountering fast-charging requirement. On the other hand, the unsatisfactory compatibility between GPE/cathode and GPE/anode interfaces deteriorates battery performance, impedes the extensive application of polymer electrolytes. Interfacial compatibility is typically described as chemical and electrochemical stability (thermodynamic stability), SEI/CEI layers formation (kinetic stability), and physical contact at the interface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0008_R_d123539657e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">8</a></sup></span> Specifically, narrow electrochemical window blocked matching between electrolyte and high-voltage cathodes. Cathode electrolyte interface layer (CEI) and the solid electrolyte interface on the anode (SEI) largely determine the interfacial stability. Loose and unstable CEI/SEI increase the interfacial barrier, inducing uneven Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. Meanwhile, uneven electrolyte's morphology and large surface tension between GPE and Na anode give rise to local current accumulation and uncontrollable Na dendrites. What's even less ideal is that notorious mechanical strength of GPE cannot effectively suppress the spread of Na dendrites during the cycling process, thereby endangering battery safety. All of the above are the key enablers for compatible interface. Therefore, developing high-Na<sup>+</sup>-conduction and highly-interfacial-compatibility GPEs still remain a complex and long-lasting challenge.</p>
         
         <p>Ionic conductivity is dominated by ion charge (c<sub>i</sub>), carrier concentration (q<sub>i</sub>) and carrier mobility (μ<sub>i</sub>), where μ<sub>i</sub> is closely related to glass transition temperature (T<sub>g</sub>), lower T<sub>g</sub> typically enhances segment motion/ion conduction. q<sub>i</sub> is an important factor determining ion conductivity, but this influence is complex due to anions and cations are charge carriers, the interaction between anions and cations cannot be ignored. Extensive strategies are proposed to improve the Na-ion conduction ability of GPEs, grafting groups onto polymer chains to increase Na<sup>+</sup> transfer sites,<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0009_R_d123539657e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Phys. Chem. C">9</a></sup></span> using plasticizing additives to reduce T<sub>g</sub> in polymer chain segments,<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0010_R_d123539657e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">10</a></sup></span> adding fillers to construct additional carrier transport pathway,<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0011_R_d123539657e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funt. Mater.">11</a></sup></span> synthesizing novel monomer and macromolecular to build special bulk architectures.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0012_R_d123539657e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a>-<a href="#anie202402245-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0014_R_d123539657e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span> These dazzling strategies improve the effect to a certain extent for reinforced ion conduction ability. However, in order to increase to 10<sup>−2</sup>–10<sup>−3</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup> that is comparable to LE and ceramic electrolytes,<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0015_R_d123539657e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Mater.">15</a></sup></span> the existing ion conductivity status of GPEs is still not insufficient and need to be further improved. In addition, the interface incompatibility is caused by many complex triggers, which make current research still insufficient to fully meet the requirements.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0016_R_d123539657e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a></sup></span> For these purposes, designing a GPE that achieves ultrafast ion transport while simultaneously achieves interfacial compatibility is essential to the development of solid-state sodium batteries.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, NaF was introduced into PLA fibers (salt-in-polymer) by electrospinning and subsequent in situ gel forming process to obtain high-Na<sup>+</sup>-conduction GPE. The NaF effectively promotes the motion of chain segments. NaF-dissolved Na<sup>+</sup> and F<sup>−</sup> renders the generation of aggerated solvation structure, and derives an inorganic-rich and conductivity-improved interface. Nanochannels generated in fibers during the swelling process in amorphous region provide increased Na<sup>+</sup> transfer pathways, so that high ion conductivity and large Na<sup>+</sup> transference number were achieved, while maintaining good mechanical properties due to the non-destructive damage to rigid crystalline region. Originating from the benefits from electrolyte bulk phase toward high-quality CEI/SEI, highly compatible gel-polymer electrolyte was obtained.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202402245-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202402245-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>A PLA-NaF membrane (not soak in the electrolyte) was synthesized via electrospinning a mixed solution containing NaF, trichloromethane (TCM) and N, N-Dimethylacetamide (DMAc). The resulting 33 cm×17 cm membrane was composed of fibers with a diameter of 1.5 μm (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0001">1a</a>–<a href="#anie202402245-fig-0001">b</a>), and its diverse shapes demonstrates its excellent flexibility (Figure S1). C and O elements represent the skeleton of PLA fiber, Na and F are distributed into the fiber networks (Figure S2a–c), 4.5 wt % NaF (Thermogravimetric curves (TG)) was introduced into PLA-NaF membrane. Meanwhile, the PLA and PLA-NaF GPEs show similar spectra, the characteristic peak of −C=O at 1748.9 cm<sup>−1</sup> and the −C−O−C stretching vibration peak at 1085.5 cm<sup>−1</sup> were observed (Figure S3), indicating that the introduced NaF does not affect polymer structure. The diffraction peaks at 38.8° and 56.1° are indexed to the (200) and (220) peaks in XRD (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0001">1d</a>) and confirmed the existence of NaF phase. After being swelled with electrolyte, the (220) peaks clearly weaker than PLA-NaF membrane, this represents a decrease in the NaF, which may be attributed to the slight dissolution of NaF. Compared to PLA and PLA-NaF white membranes, the morphology of PLA-NaF membrane became transparent after going through swelling process (Inset ①–③). When PLA-NaF membrane was exposed to 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> EC/PC with 5wt % FEC for 2 days, PLA-NaF membrane still maintain intact size (Figure S4). Originating from the dipole interactions among C=O bonds in EC, PC, FEC, and C=O at the end of PLA polymer chains, PLA-NaF membrane can become gel state in most ester- and ether-based electrolytes (EC/PC/FEC, EC/DMC, EC/DEC/FEC, and DGM, Figure S5), presenting excellent compatibility and chemical stability.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202402245-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/19ee78ee-89ea-4b45-a6eb-b32da35bbb9d/anie202402245-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/19ee78ee-89ea-4b45-a6eb-b32da35bbb9d/anie202402245-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a871e129-c272-439c-ab2d-ebeb39449860/anie202402245-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/19ee78ee-89ea-4b45-a6eb-b32da35bbb9d/anie202402245-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202402245-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202402245" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Preparation and physical characterization of PLA-NaF membrane. (a) Preparation process of PLA-NaF membrane. (b) SEM image of PLA-NaF membrane and elements distribution of C, O, Na and F. (c) TG curves of PLA membrane and PLA-NaF membrane. (d) XRD profiles of PLA, PLA-NaF membrane and PLA-NaF GPE. Inset: digital images of PLA, PLA-NaF membrane and PLA-NaF GPE. (e) Cryo-TEM image of electrolyte-swelling PLA-NaF fiber. (f) SEM image of swelling PLA-NaF fiber. (g) Dipole interaction mechanism between PLA polymer chains and EC, PC and FEC molecules. (h) 3D Cryo-TEM tomography of swelling PLA-NaF fiber. (i) Forming process of PLA-NaF GPE monitored by quartz crystal microbalance. The mass change is calculated according to a formula: ▵m=−C▵f/n, ▵m is the mass change of PLA-NaF membrane, C is a constant of 17.7 ng Hz<sup>−1</sup>cm<sup>−2</sup>, n is the overtone of the oscillations (<i>n</i>=3 for the analyzed data), and ▵f is the resonance frequency change.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The initial PLA-NaF fiber is solid (Cryo-TEM, Figure S6). After swelling with electrolyte, the luffa-shaped porous fibers are formed (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0001">1e</a>, in which, the black part in the middle of the fiber is caused by the overlapping two porous fibers), this is due to the dipole interactions between C=O in amorphous regions with loose chain segments and solvents, resulting the chain segments in amorphous regions are prone to softening, collapsing and ultimately forming a porous structure (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0001">1f</a>–<a href="#anie202402245-fig-0001">g</a> and Figure S7) along with a gel state.</p>
         
         <p>Additionally, a detailed 3 D interior structure is revealed by Cryo-TEM tomography. In Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0001">1h</a>, the domain in violet region is the PLA polymer matrix (rigid framework of PLA-NaF GPE), and the black voids in it represent nanopores in the fiber interior (ion channel), confirming the formation of 3D consecutive nanopores for ion transport in the fiber bulk. The quartz crystal microbalance reveals the dynamic swelling process of PLA-NaF membrane, when PLA-NaF membrane was soaked in LE, Δ f<sub>3</sub> reached a plateau with small fluctuations after 6 minutes, which is mainly attributed to the finish of electrolyte absorption and nanopore formation processes (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0001">1i</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0017_R_d123539657e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Energy">17</a></sup></span> Specifically, the resonance frequency change (Δ f<sub>3</sub>) decreased from 1796.22 Hz to 1784.41 Hz and accessed a balance stage within 6 minutes, signifying that PLA-NaF membrane have experienced a mass increase of 69.62 ng cm<sup>−2</sup> owing to the balance between electrolyte absorption and pore formation processes. Compared with nonpolar PP separator, the C=O groups in PLA chains have excellent affinity with most electrolytes (EC/PC/FEC, DGM and EC/DEC), resulting in PLA-NaF membrane with a contact angle of nearly 0° (Figure S8), exhibiting excellent electrolyte affinity. Meanwhile, unique porous fibers of PLA-NaF GPE increase the storage space of the electrolyte (EC/PC/FEC), and PLA-NaF GPE shows a higher swelling rate than LE (320 %) and commercial PP separator (Figure S9). Nonetheless, they still exhibit high safety in combustion tests because of the efficient encapsulation and fixation capabilities of the polymer skeleton for solvents, making the battery much safer. In turn, PP separator immediately burns and produces flames (Figure S10). Moreover, the NaF salt can reduce the T<sub>g</sub> of PLA polymer from 83.4 °C to 77.5 °C. Such results imply the enhanced chain segments motion and ion conduction ability (Figure S11), in accordance with the rate capacity of PLA GPE and PLA-NaF GPE (Figure S12a–b).</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical window of PLA-NaF GPE exhibits an oxidative stability of 4.97 V (versus. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), which is higher than that of LE (4.81 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), indicating its potential for matching high-voltage cathode materials (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0002">2a</a>). The PLA-NaF GPE shows better mechanical strength (1.62 MPa) than GF (0.53 MPa) and the previously reported works (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure S13). After immersed PLA-NaF membrane and GF in electrolyte (1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> EC/PC with 5 wt % FEC), PLA-NaF GPE still displays an enhanced tensile strength (2.43 MPa) than laboratory-based GF (0.41 MPa). The enhanced tensile strength of PLA-NaF GPE after immersed electrolyte is attributed that the dipole interaction occurs in their amorphous region, without damaging the rigid frameworks. At the same time, abundant intermolecular interactions generate between -C=O in EC/PC/FEC molecules and −C=O in amorphous region of PLA polymers, which greatly increase the mechanical stability of electrolyte-immersed PLA-NaF membrane. Excellent mechanical property endows PLA-NaF GPE with the potential to prevent damage caused by external collision and suppress Na dendrites.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0018_R_d123539657e718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#anie202402245-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0019_R_d123539657e718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202402245-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/642309c5-d7c7-4006-ba4b-fb4c94e2bf22/anie202402245-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/642309c5-d7c7-4006-ba4b-fb4c94e2bf22/anie202402245-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/effd0450-ebdc-41f1-9c63-23db70aad426/anie202402245-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/642309c5-d7c7-4006-ba4b-fb4c94e2bf22/anie202402245-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202402245-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202402245" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Physicochemical and Na<sup>+</sup> conduction properties. (a) Linear scanning voltammetry curves. Inset: comparison of electrochemical widows. (b) Stress-strain curves. (c) Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy plots of stainless steel || stainless steel (SS). (d) Temperature-dependent ion conductivity. (e) Chronoamperometry profiles of LE and PLA-NaF GPE in Na || Na symmetric cells. (f) Ion conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> transference number comparison of PLA-NaF GPE with LE and reported works.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>PLA-NaF GPE exhibits a much higher ion conductivity (2.50×10<sup>−3</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>, calculated according to Equation (1)) than 1.77×10<sup>−3</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup> of LE at 25 °C (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0002">2c</a>), error bars come from three cell (Figure S14–S15 and Table S1–S2). Moreover, the activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) of the PLA-NaF GPE (6.11 kJ/mol) extracted from the temperature dependence of ionic conductivity from 30 °C to 70 °C was lower than 10.50 kJ/mol of LE (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0002">2d</a>), and Na<sup>+</sup> owns lower energy barriers than the LE, relevant formula and data are presented in Equation (2) and Table S3. The Na<sup>+</sup> transference number of PLA-NaF GPE increases from 0.61 to 0.75, compared with that of the LE (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0002">2e</a> and Figure S16a-b, calculated according to Equation (3)). The ion conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> transference number outperform LE counterparts and most of the reported gel-electrolytes (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0002">2f</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0020_R_d123539657e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20">20</a></sup></span> As tracked by molecular dynamics (MD) simulations in Figure S17, PLA-NaF GPE system demonstrates larger Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of PLA-NaF GPE (D<sub>PLA-NaF GPE</sub>=4.70×10<sup>−7</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, 298.15 K), which is a little higher than that of. LE (D<sub>LE</sub>=4.46×10<sup>−7</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, 298.15 K). Fortunately, PLA-NaF GPE displays good mechanical properties for the non-destructive rigid support, therefore, it achieves the balance between mechanical properties and electrical properties (Figure S18).</p>
         
         <p>The practical application of PLA-NaF GPE is examined in sodium batteries. The Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell delivers a capacity of 114.1 mAh/g after 300 cycles, with 95.0 % capacity retention at 25 °C (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0003">3a</a>). By contrast, the capacity of Na|LE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell is 79.5 mAh/g after 300 cycles (69.7 %). The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) at initial and 50 cycles at 5 C shows that Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell exhibits smaller interfacial impendence (688.7 Ω) at pristine than that of Na|LE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell (966.8 Ω). After 50 cycles at 5 C, Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell still exhibits smaller interfacial impendence (93.0 Ω) than that of Na|LE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell (232.7 Ω), indicating good interfacial contact and favorable interface for Na<sup>+</sup> transfer (Figure S19). Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0003">3b</a> demonstrates the rate performance of the Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell at a potential range of 2.5–4.0 V. It delivered specific capacities of 111.6, 109.3, 107.8, 105.9, and 104.8 mAh/g at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C, which are substantially higher than those of the liquid cell and surpass those previously reported electrolytes (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0003">3c</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0027_R_d123539657e879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21">21</a></sup></span> Notably, PLA-NaF GPE could match Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode with varying loadings (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0003">3d</a>). As the loading and areal capacity increases from 1.1 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> to 3.3 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, 5.2 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, 7.3 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, 9.1 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> and 13.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, the close overpotentials and reversible capacity (111.6 mAh/g to 107.3 mAh/g, 107.3 mAh/g, 107.9 mAh/g, 107.8 mAh/g and 107.7 mAh/g) are achieved. The change tendency of capacity is consistent with the EIS (Figure S20). As the loading increases, the charge transfer impedance between Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> particles increases. It further proves the excellent kinetics of PLA-NaF GPE assembled cells and practical application potential.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202402245-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bdb954c2-f14c-4446-a1fb-91faba8d5a8e/anie202402245-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/bdb954c2-f14c-4446-a1fb-91faba8d5a8e/anie202402245-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4fac6315-e126-47a5-b20f-aeb09618658c/anie202402245-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bdb954c2-f14c-4446-a1fb-91faba8d5a8e/anie202402245-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202402245-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202402245" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance of the PLA-NaF GPE in sodium batteries. (a) Cycle performance of Na || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell at 2.5 V-4.0 V, 25 °C, 0.2 C. Error bars come from three cells. (b) Capacities at various current densities. (c) Comparative rate performance in this work with that of recently reported works. (d) Galvanostatic charge/discharge voltage profiles of PLA-NaF GPE assembled Na || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell with different loadings. (e) Cycle performance of Na || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell at 10 C, 60 °C. (f) Capacities at various current densities and temperatures. (g) Galvanostatic charge/discharge voltage profiles of Na || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> pouch cell assembled with PLA-NaF GPE at 0.5 C. Inset: Digital images of pouch cell. (h) Digital images of the pouch cell lighting up LED bulbs under normal, cutting, cutting and folding conditions.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The effectiveness of the PLA-NaF GPE at high-temperature and low-temperatures was explored. The Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell exhibits 73.9 % capacity retention after 1350 cycles at 5 C. While, that of liquid cell delivered rapidly enlarged capacity loss (45.7 %, Figure S21). Under 10 C at 60 °C (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0003">3e</a>), the Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell retains 100 mAh/g after 250 cycles. Conversely, the liquid cells fade quickly (80 mAh/g after 250 cycles). The low-temperatures performance of PLA-NaF GPE was also tested at 0.2 C in Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0003">3f</a>, the Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell displays the discharged capacities of 105.2, 99.2, 88.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0 °C, −15 °C, −25 °C, and remains 57.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at −35 °C. This demonstrated its high feasibility for use in extremely low-temperatures.</p>
         
         <p>Next, Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> pouch cell (negative/positive (N/P) ratio=3.2) was assembled, and it delivers a discharge capacity of 9.2 mAh at the second cycle with an energy density of 105.0 wh/kg (based on the mass of active materials in cathode and anode, Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0003">3g</a>), and a specific capacity retention of around 100 % after 10 cycles at 25 °C were obtained (Figure S22). Additionally, the Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> pouch cell can light up 24 light emitting diode (LED) bulbs under the normal, cutting, cutting and folding conditions, demonstrating the safety of the device assembled by PLA-NaF GPE (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0003">3h</a>). To verify the compatibility of PLA-NaF GPE with different cathode and anode materials and the universality of this “salt-in-polymer” strategy. The synthesized PLA-NaF GPE has been expanded to Na || Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, (high voltage cathode materials, 2.0 V–4.5 V, Figure S23a–b), Na || Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.32</sub>Mn<sub>0.68</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (Figure S24a–b), Na || NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Figure S25), Na || FeS<sub>2</sub>@N, S−C (Figure S26a–b), and HC || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> battery systems (Figure S27a–b), and various polymer systems (PVDF-HFP, Figure S28a-b, and PVP, Figure S28c–d). Even in lithium battery systems, PLA-NaF GPE assembled Li || LiFePO<sub>4</sub> cells still has good compatibility and cycle performance (Figure S29a–b).</p>
         
         <p>The role of NaF salt in the GPE was finely studied. The solubility experiment (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0004">4a</a>) indicates that the solubility of NaF in EC/PC/FEC, PC and FEC is 1.03 g/L, 4.07 g/L and 4.94 g/L at 25 °C, respectively. Due to EC presents solid state at 25 °C, the solubility of NaF in EC is 1.57 g/L at 35 °C. Turbid solvents in inset demonstrate the dissolved behavior of NaF in organic solvents (Table S3).<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0032_R_d123539657e1082" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#anie202402245-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0033_R_d123539657e1082" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span> The snapshots of the LE and PLA-NaF GPE treated LE equilibrated systems are shown in Figure S30 and Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0004">4b</a>, added Na<sup>+</sup> and F<sup>−</sup> ions are dispersed in PLA-NaF GPE system, which confirms the dissolution of NaF in electrolyte. Accordingly, the function NaF-dissolved Na<sup>+</sup> and F<sup>−</sup> in electrolyte was investigated. FT-IR spectra performs that the typical absorption peak of C=O blue-shifts from 1751.8 cm<sup>−1</sup> to 1771.2 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0004">4c</a>). It is suggested that added Na<sup>+</sup> or F<sup>−</sup> were introduced into the solvation structure for their dissolution in solvents and causes an uneven charge distribution.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0034_R_d123539657e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span> The peak of −C−F in <sup>19</sup>F NMR shows an upshift, indicating that F atomic nucleus is shielded by a dense electron cloud (Figure S31), this result is consistent with the FT-IR spectra. Subsequently, Raman spectra screens a clear blue-shift owing to the strong interaction between solvent molecules and NaF species (Figure S32), which indicates that more free solvents are more inclined towards coordination modes. Raman characteristics peaks contributed by ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> for the bare electrolyte, PLA-NaF GPE and PLA-NaF GPE treated electrolyte are deconvoluted with constituent peak area ratios (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0004">4d</a>). Lower SSIP of PLA-NaF GPE (25.3 %) than bare electrolyte (32.5 %) and higher AGG of PLA-NaF GPE (42.3 %, ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> &gt;1) than bare electrolyte (32.8 %) represents dissolved Na<sup>+</sup> can concentrate electrolyte and drive more AGG generation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0035_R_d123539657e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Mater.">25</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202402245-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/db4d9e6f-af45-48e4-9593-90006c346484/anie202402245-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/db4d9e6f-af45-48e4-9593-90006c346484/anie202402245-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/88cbde59-b76d-429b-a3c2-4edee4677673/anie202402245-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/db4d9e6f-af45-48e4-9593-90006c346484/anie202402245-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202402245-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202402245" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Analysis of NaF's effect on solvation structure. (a) Solubility of NaF in EC, PC, FEC and EC/PC/FEC solvents. Inset: Digital images of NaF dissolves in solvents. (b) Snapshots of Na<sup>+</sup> and corresponding chemical environments in the equilibrium state of PLA-NaF GPE treated electrolyte. (c) FT-IR spectra of bare electrolyte and PLA-NaF GPE. (d–e) Raman spectra of constituents of PLA-NaF GPE (SSIP: separated ion pairs, CIP: contact ion pairs, AGG: aggregated ion pairs). (f) Radical distribution function and the comparison of coordination number for LE and PLA-NaF GPE. (g) Schematic representation of NaF-solubility regulates solvation structure. In situ Raman spectra (h) and in situ UV/Vis spectra (i) of PLA-NaF GPE assembled Na || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell during the initial cycling process, respectively.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Using MD, we obtained the average coordinate number (CN) of Na by analyzing the radial distribution function in PLA-NaF treated electrolyte and bare electrolyte systems. Na<sup>+</sup> has average CN of 0.301 ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>, 0.478 EC, 0.458 PC, and 0.030 FEC (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0004">4f</a>), which are slightly higher than 0.202, 0.418, 0.441, and 0.027 in LE system (Figure S33a-b). Of note, the dissolution of NaF shortens the distance of the peak toward the center of Na<sup>+</sup> (Na<sup>+</sup>-O (ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>)) from 0.307 Å, 0.368 Å to 0.301 Å and 0.364 Å, indicating ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> closer integration with Na<sup>+</sup>. Meanwhile, NaF-added electrolyte exhibits an enhanced g(r) of Na<sup>+</sup>-O (ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>) pairs in the first solvated sheath, that is say there is an increased interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>. Also, the CN of Na<sup>+</sup>-O (ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>) increases from 0.202 to 0.301 (Figure S33c), when the CN increases by 5 times to the actual number, the CN of Na<sup>+</sup> has increased from 1 to 1.5 and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> becomes a structure shared by two solvent sheaths. Dissolved NaF leads to added AGG structure (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0004">4g</a>), which is firmly demonstrated by experiments, characterization and MD. Interestingly, in situ Raman spectra showcases the varying peak at 540 cm<sup>−1</sup> (characteristic peak of NaF powder, Figure S34, Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0004">4h</a>), which gradually weakens and then strengthens during the initial charge and discharge process. The phenomenon indicates that the charge process increases the disorder of electrolyte system and promotes the dissolution of NaF in electrolyte. Subsequently, electrolyte’ decompose posed the salt is in a supersaturated state, resulting in the precipitation of NaF. Simultaneously, in situ UV-Vis spectra suggests that a new peak (216 nm, emerges due to the addition of NaF, Figure S35, Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0004">4i</a>) has increased during the charge process and decreased in the discharge process, which complements with in situ Raman spectra.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0036_R_d123539657e1222" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Energy Lett.">26</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The formation of AGG structure changes the electronic structure and redox stability of electrolyte components, which cause changes in interphase composition.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0037_R_d123539657e1227" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nat. Mater.">27</a></sup></span> The HOMO (the highest occupied orbital) energy levels of different molecules and clusters are calculated via the density functional theory (DFT). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0005">5a</a>, the HOMO energy levels of the SSIPs, CIP and AGG are −6.460 eV, −6.414 eV, and −6.225 eV. Therefore, PLA-NaF GPE system with more AGG (ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup>) are easier to preferential decomposition and form a inorganic-rich CEI. Cryo-TEM uncovers that the CEI formed in the Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell is approximately 8.4 nm, which is thinner than 11.9 nm formed in liquid cell (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0005">5b</a>–<a href="#anie202402245-fig-0005">c</a>). Therefore, subsequent evolutions of thickness in Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> were monitored, after 1 cycle and 3 cycles, the CEI are slightly increase to 8.7 and 9.7 nm (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0005">5d</a>–<a href="#anie202402245-fig-0005">e</a>). The C−C/C=C, C−O, and C=O species in C1s spectrum were assigned to ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na, RCOONa, and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, which are mainly derived from the decomposition of EC, PC and FEC. More ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>-derived NaCl (51.6 %) in Cl 2p XPS spectra were detected on the PLA-NaF GPE assembled Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> surface rather than LE (11.4 %). The NaF (16.2 %) on Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> surface is still higher than LE system (14.3 %) (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0005">5f</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0038_R_d123539657e1299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">28</a>, <a href="#anie202402245-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0039_R_d123539657e1299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Commun.">29</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the EIS of Na || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> at different electrochemical states upon initial charge process (Figure S36) shows that PLA-NaF GPE assembled cell has the smaller interfacial impendence compared with LE after charge to 4.0 V, suggesting that a high ion transport interface is formed.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202402245-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/612fc2de-6827-430b-96b3-ff9179bdcb15/anie202402245-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/612fc2de-6827-430b-96b3-ff9179bdcb15/anie202402245-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/27fc38bb-85f7-4afc-9775-62c06f3a8810/anie202402245-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/612fc2de-6827-430b-96b3-ff9179bdcb15/anie202402245-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202402245-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202402245" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Interfacial chemistry in PLA-NaF GPE assembled Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode side. (a) HOMO energy levels of solvents and different solvation structure. (b–c) Cryo-TEM of Na || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cells assembled with LE and PLA-NaF GPE at initially charging to 4.0 V. (d–e) Cryo-TEM of Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cells after 1 cycle and 3 cycles. (f) C1s, F1s and Cl2p XPS spectra of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> surface after charge to 4.0 V at 0.1 C. (g) Electrochemical quartz crystal microbalance of LE and PLA-NaF GPE assembled Na ||  Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cells. (h) OLEMS of liquid cell and Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell. SEM images of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> powder in LE (i) and PLA-NaF GPE (j) after 80 cycles.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Electrochemical quartz crystal microbalance (EQCM) was employed to detected the mass change of the chips loaded with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathodes (ensuring consistent quality of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>). As the Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0005">5g</a> showcased, the mass of CEI in Na|LE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell is 1.910 μg/cm<sup>2</sup> heavier than that of Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> after 1 cycle, and 0.413 μg/cm<sup>2</sup> heavier than CEI in Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell after charging to 4.0 V. A heavier CEI formed on Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> surface, this result is consistent with the results obtained by Cryo-TEM, signifying that the PLA-NaF GPE assembled cells have stable, dense and inorganic-rich CEI that can effectively passivate the cathode surface to avoid side reactions between the electrode and the electrolyte. On-line electrochemical mass spectroscopy (OLEMS, Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0005">5h</a>) provides a detailed evolution for the gas production behavior during the cycling. As the charging process progresses from 2.5 V to 4.0 V, unstable EC undergoes oxidative decomposition and produces CO<sub>2</sub>. The liquid cell yields a large amount of CO<sub>2</sub> (1.580 μL) at 4.75 V, and there is no obvious CO<sub>2</sub> production in the PLA-NaF GPE assembled cell during the charging process (0.099 μL). H<sub>2</sub> are the main gaseous products of various side effects (H<sub>2</sub>O decomposition, protonated EC decomposition and side reactions between binder PVDF and Na metal). The solid-state sodium battery assembled with PLA-NaF GPE has minimal H<sub>2</sub> (1.180 μL) at 1.43 h compared with the liquid cell at 0.43 h (4.740 μL). More importantly, the production time delays from 0.43 h to 1.43 h.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0040_R_d123539657e1472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">30</a>, <a href="#anie202402245-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0041_R_d123539657e1472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Commun.">31</a></sup></span> Robust CEI can effectively covering Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and prevent the particle breakage during long-term cycling process, thus endowing the electrode with high structural integrity (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0005">5i</a>). On the contrary, LE assembled Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> undergoes crushing after 80 cycles at 0.2 C, and increased area and loose CEI can further exacerbate the occurrence of side reactions and deteriorate battery performance (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0005">5j</a>).</p>
         
         <p>Accordingly, the interface features between electrolyte and Na anode have also been explored. Surface roughness can pose inhomogeneous current distribution across the rough surface, and worsen the stability between Na metal and electrolyte interface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0042_R_d123539657e1502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Energy Environ. Sci.">32</a></sup></span> PLA-NaF membrane has a smoother and flat morphology than GF (Atomic force microscope, Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0006">6a</a>–<a href="#anie202402245-fig-0006">b</a>). ensuring its good contact with Na anode and beneficial for reducing local accumulation of current. Meanwhile, the compatibility between melted Na and electrolyte were tested in Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0006">6c</a>, the PLA-NaF GPE delivers lower contact angle with Na anode (55°) than GF (79°). The low surface tension suppresses the possibility of dendritic growth by reducing local current density. Good mechanical strength avoids the piercing of Na dendrites during long cycling process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202402245-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202402245-bib-0043_R_d123539657e1514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">33</a></sup></span> The LUMO (the lowest unoccupied orbital) energy levels in Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0006">6d</a> were also calculated via DFT, the LUMO energy levels of the SSIPs, CIP and AGG are −1.127 eV, −4.793 eV, −5.314 eV. The PLA-NaF GPE system with more AGG would benefit inorganic-rich SEI formation due to the lower LUMO energy. As proof of calculation, C1s, F1s and Cl2p XPS spectra of Na surface after 2 cycles at 0.1 C were collected in Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0006">6e</a>. NaF (72.5 %) was detected in PLA-NaF GPE assembled Na anode surface, which is higher than LE (69.8 %), this may be due to the NaF in electrolyte migrate onto the Na anode surface and then are encapsulated by solvent- and salt-derived SEI. Concurrently, minor organic matters decomposed by solvents, and more inorganic salt originated from sodium salt, thereby acquiring a favorable SEI for resisting Na dendrites. As shown in Figure S37, compared to Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na cell (1115.6 Ω), the Na|LE|Na cell showed an impedance over 2519.2 Ω at pristine, further indicating highly conductive interface and tight interface contact between PLA-NaF GPE and Na metal anode. The above outcomes reveal that PLA-NaF GPE can synergistically optimize the interface between electrodes/electrolyte, and entrust high compatibility between them.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202402245-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/25def7c7-56b9-4d23-9adc-5739a31ffeb3/anie202402245-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/25def7c7-56b9-4d23-9adc-5739a31ffeb3/anie202402245-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3592bd2a-56e8-44d2-a64b-d6de427b6037/anie202402245-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/25def7c7-56b9-4d23-9adc-5739a31ffeb3/anie202402245-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202402245-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202402245" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Interfacial chemistry in PLA-NaF GPE assembled Na anode side. (a–b) Atomic force microscope images of GF and PLA-NaF membrane. (c) Compatibility tests between Na and GF, PLA-NaF GPE. (d) LUMO energy levels of solvents and different solvation structure. (e) F1s, Cl2p and C1s XPS spectra of Na surface after cycling 2 cycles at 0.1 C. (f) States of the Na anode after charging to 4.0 V, 1 cycle, 2 cycles and 3 cycles at 0.1 C. (g) Galvanostatic cycling curves of Na || Na cells at a current density of 0.2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. Inset: Galvanostatic cycling curves within 404 h to 410 h and 602 h to 604 h. (h) In situ optical microscope observation of Na-dendrites growth in the LE and PLA-NaF GPE assembled Na || Na cells.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The surface morphologies of the disassembled Na metal after charging to 4.0 V at 0.1 C were displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0006">6f</a>. Note that apparent side reaction products formed on Na metal surface in Na|LE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell, and loses its original metallic luster. Nevertheless, the Na metal using the PLA-NaF GPE still maintains its original luster. Therefore, subsequent 1, 2 and 3 cycles in Na|PLA-NaF GPE|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> were monitored, and these photos also show the original metallic luster of Na metal. It indicates that favorable SEI in PLA-NaF GPE assembled cells can prevent electrolytes from excessive decomposition during cell cycling and add interfacial stability between electrolyte and Na anode. Na || Na symmetric cells assembled with LE and PLA-NaF GPE show a similar polarization voltage of 0.14 mV (Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0006">6g</a>), while, after 406 h cycling, the voltage of liquid cell decreases abruptly displaying the characteristics of hard breakdown. Conversely, PLA-NaF GPE assembled Na || Na cell still running stably after 600 h, which is profiting from excellent interface compatibility as proven above. To validate the Na deposition behavior in the LE and PLA-NaF GPE systems, in situ optical microscope of Na || Na cells are assembled. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202402245-fig-0006">6h</a>, benefitting from the excellent contact between Na and PLA-NaF GPE, and inorganic rich SEI, the PLA-NaF GPE assembled cell exhibits even Na deposition behavior. However, LE generates a large number of Na dendrites and bubbles during the deposition process, this is due to insufficient physical contact and rough contours, which causes local accumulation of charge and provides nucleation sites. In addition, loose and low strength SEI is also leads to the growth of dendrites.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202402245-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202402245-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, a Na-ion conductive gel-polymer electrolyte was obtained by electrospinning and subsequent in situ gel forming process. The obtained PLA-NaF GPE delivers high ion conductivity (2.50×10<sup>−3</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>) and Na<sup>+</sup> transference number (0.75). The synthesized PLA-NaF membrane with NaF particles inlaid in polylactide porous fiber network forms a “salt-in-polymer” gel-polymer electrolyte. Highly distributed nanochannels were found in fibers due to the dipolar interaction between −C=O in polymer and solvents, which facilitates the appearance of nanopores. These liquid-electrolyte-dominated nanopores ensure the fast transport of Na<sup>+</sup> in electrolyte bulk phase. Notably, NaF salt can dynamically dissolves in EC/PC/FEC electrolyte (1.03 g/L), and the generated Na<sup>+</sup> and F<sup>−</sup> ions lead to concentrated electrolyte environment via coordinated with free solvents molecules, which promotes the aggregates-dominated Na<sup>+</sup> solvation sheath (ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>/solvents &gt;1). The aggregates with higher HOMO inducing salt-rich CEI on Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with thickness of 8.4 nm (Cryo-TEM), which is clearly thinner than that of electrolyte without gel forming (11.9 nm, Cryo-TEM). EQCM result displays that this CEI mass is 42.1 % of that from liquid electrolyte cell, is agreement with the above-mentioned outcomes. Simultaneously, delightful SEI formed due to the low LUMO, smooth contour and low surface tension. Exceptional compatibility between PLA-NaF GPE and Na anode ensures a dendrites-free Na anode, improved the safety of Na metal-based battery. As a result, the assembled Na || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> button cells display excellent electrochemical performance at 25 °C (92.4 %, 650 cycles at 0.2 C), 60 °C (92.2 %, 250 cycles at 10 C) and −35 °C (57.3 mAh/g, 0.2 C), has demonstrated excellent performance over a wide temperature range. Even at the loading of 13.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, it also presents high capacity of 107.7 mAh/g. Moreover, the assembled pouch cell displays reliable safety under the cutting and folding conditions, and the PLA-NaF GPE demonstrates powerful compatibility with various electrode materials (Na || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na || Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na || Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.32</sub>Mn<sub>0.68</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na || NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na || FeS<sub>2</sub>@N, S−C and HC || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>). The universality of this “salt-in-polymer” gel-polymer electrolyte can be extended to other polymer systems.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
